# 246\n
{Cambridge, Trinity College B.14.39 (323), hand A: fols. 19r, 25rb, 25v, 27rb, 28r-29v, 32r-33v, 36r-46r, 47r-v, 83v-84r}\n
C13b1\n
366 255 N\n
{=E Herefords=}\n
{=Sample represents all the English in this hand=}\n
{=Script - Textura semiquadrata with some cursive tendencies=}\n
{=Status - MS punctuation done; tagging notes and textual notes up to date=}\n
{=Note that in this scribe\'s script the letter V often has the second\n
stroke meet the first only about half-way down instead of near the foot.\n
In these cases it is tempting to read it as a wynn, but it appears in\n
such a form in the Latin text in VIGILI and the second part of the usual\n
ligatured W also matches it in shape, so CB13 and Reichl (1973) are certainly\n
right to read it as V. Note also that although y and Y are distinguished\n
in shape, y quite often appears for (consonantal) Y = /j/, and for\n
vocalic Y (especially in diphthongs), but Y does not appear for\n
consonantal y. N.B. DEYyEN "die" inf. From 29r, and especially from fol.\n
33v, wynn is often used instead of more usual W, but is always\n
thorn-shaped (cf. w/y confusion in Cotton Caligula Aix). But N.B.\n
SYIKEL once. The shapes of V, Y and y are variable enough and similar\n
enough for it not always to be possible to decide which is which. z\n
almost never used - not apparently in this scribe\'s normal inventory.\n
There is one clear example in zAF and yogh is there 5-shaped (cf. also\n
HAI[z]EN which may have yogh. Contrast HONKz "hang" vps23 with Latin/French\n
abbreviation for -ET correctly used=}\n
{~f19r~}\n
{para}\n
{=The paragraph markers used in Scribe A\'s texts are usually the\n
gallows-shaped paragraphus, but sometimes the C-shaped paraph is used.\n
Sometimes it seems that that Scribe A himself has put in paragraphus\n
and a later rubricator covers it with another or a paraph. All kinds are\n
here noted as para=} \n

\'_*ONFREIS\n
{=This is clearly written as one word and is the right spelling for the\n
French personal name (Humphrey in English) not, as Reichl (1973)\n
suggests, the phrase "on fresh (i.e. young) children"=}\n
$child/npl_CHILDREN\n
$be/vps23_BEID\n
{=In this scribe\'s writing system EI in stressed syllables almost always\n
indicates the reflex of a long vowel or diphthong - [ae:], [e:], [e:o],\n
[e:a], etc. Most commonly it represents the reflex of OE [e:o]. I think\n
it unlikely therefore that it here represents "bed" as Reichl (1973)\n
suggests. The final letter could represent the reflex of earlier [t] or\n
[d] in this scribe\'s system. The plausible candidates are few: OE be:ot\n
"boast" is attractive because of the reference to boasting in the last\n
line of the verse. But it is hard to see how the verb "strip" could be\n
used in relation to it. OE be:od "table, board" would perhaps be more\n
likely. But the simplest solution seems to be to take BEID as the\n
finite verb. Although BEID is a unique spelling for "are" in this\n
scribe\'s system, BEIT is his commonest spelling for it, and elsewhere\n
final D and T are freely interchanged. This interpretation then makes\n
it possible to keep Onfrei\'s children as the subject till the end of\n
the verse with HEO being for "they" (the commonest spelling) rather\n
than for "he" (used, but much more rarely)=}\n
$/P23N_HEO\n
$strip/vpp-pl_STRUP+TE $/vpp[P]-pl_+TE\n
{"robbed"}\n
$forsooth/av{rh}_FOR-SOIT $sooth/av-k_-SOIT\n
{.\'}\n
{\\}\n
$at/pr_*AT\n
$evensong/n<pr_EUE+SONC $song/n<pr-k_+SONC\n
$amorn/av_A-MOREWEN $a-/xp-av_A-\n
{"the next next day"}\n
$well{v}/av_WEL\n
$little/av_LUTEL\n
$there/av_yER\n
$/P23N_HEO\n
{=It seems more logical to take this HEO to refer to Onfrei\'s children\n
rather than to the thief=}\n
$louen{b}/vps23F-apn{rh}_LOI+T $/vps23[V]F-apn{rh}_+T\n
{="boast, congratulate themselves". In other words, Onfrei\'s children\n
boast little (i.e. they lament), presumably because they have been\n
"stripped, robbed". Formally this verb cannot be from either lotien\n
or hloYian as Reichl (1973) suggests, but must be from a monosyllabic\n
vowel stem (cf. GOIT below). It can only plausibly be from AF loer,\n
louer. See MED s.v. louen v. (4), not with the usual (cited) sense\n
"praise" but with the sense, "boast" present in Anglo-French (see\n
AND s.v. louer). This little verse remains rather obscure, but this\n
interpretation makes syntactic sense, fits formally with the scribe\'s\n
idiosyncratic writing system and is the best I can suggest. The\n
subject may perhaps have some topical reference to the de Bohun\n
family, Earls of Hereford (both the second earl (ca. 1208-1275)\n
and his grandson, the third earl (ca. 1249-1299) were called\n
Humphrey) but this is pure conjecture=}\n
{.}\n

$god/nOd_GOID\n
{=sic. This form translates Latin DomiNUM above, so must be for the word\n
"god"; but the spelling is more usual in this scribe\'s usage rather for\n
"good" where the I can be explained as a diacritic indicating length. In\n
this context the spelling could perhaps be an error for "good" triggered by the\n
word "evil" immediately preceding. But cf. *MI GOID & MI LOUerD below\n
without any such trigger. Also note that this scribe has digraph\n
spellings for short and even unstressed vowels elsewhere in his system=}\n

$/P12Oi_yE\n
$give/vSpt13_zAF\n
{=The yogh is a very unfamiliar figura for this littera. It begins with a\n
curved stroke well above the baseline, as if to form a C, E or T above\n
the line. From this a vertical stroke is drawn to the baseline. The\n
final shape is exactly like one of the commonly used shapes for\n
Arabic figure 5 in medieval times. Arabic numbers were not yet in\n
very common use in England at this time - though note Dan Michel\'s\n
extensive use of them in the structuring of Ayenbite. Moreover, if\n
this is transferred use of a number-shape for a letter, 5 for yogh\n
is (as far as I know) unique. Reichl (1973) makes no comment about\n
the figura. It is perhaps more likely to be an adaptation to textura\n
script of a rune for the littera \'ge\', so called in the runic alphabet\n
preserved on fol. 2r of the Ormulum MS in the hand of the contemporary\n
scribe who supplied the cues to the Latin Gospel pericopes. The runic\n
shape has a vertical stroke and a left to right horizontal stroke\n
followed by another vertical stroke rather than having the upper curved\n
stroke, but the version here is a plausible adaptation for running\n
script. My attention was drawn to this alphabet in the Ormulum MS by\n
Nils-Lennart Johannesson in a presentation in Murcia, Spain in May 2013=}\n
$free/av{rh}_FREO\n
{.\'}\n

word, and the final surviving letter is not fully clear, but could I\n
think be a T. The meaning requires a past participle meaning "pierced"\n
(with the augur). This would be an early citation in English for a\n
verb from the noun "spear", but cf. AND s.v. espeer which shows that\n
the meaning was present in AF=}\n

$toad/n{rh}_CADDE\n
{=sic. C for T, as in the inflection of the verbs "prick" and "stick"\n
below. C and T are quite distinct in this hand - T having a clear\n
horizontal capping cross bar. The top of C is rounded and drawn down.\n
Laing 1997 takes this word to be a form of "caddis", but given the\n
occasional confusion in this hand between \'c\' and \'t\' it seems preferable\n
to follow the Latin above, to which this part of the English\n
corresponds, and read "toad" translating BUFO. I owe this suggestion to\n
Peter Petre=}\n

$all/av_AL\n
$about{p}/pr_A+BUT+ $a-/xp-pr_A+\n
$/P13<prM_+IM\n
{=Carleton Brown reads this as ABUTUN, presumably taking the word simply\n
as "about", although this would be a unique form as far as I know. In\n
fact -UN is not apparently what the scribe actually wrote. Scribe A\n
does not tend to join his minim strokes for \'n\', \'m\', \'u\' either at\n
the top or foot, but here the first minim stroke of the four is\n
clearly marked with an oblique stroke, the scribe\'s usual indication\n
for \'i\'. It is possible that the interlined HIM earlier in the line\n
was a hyper-correction apparently by the same scribe, as we seem to\n
have two realisations of the pronoun HIM. Other versions of \'Respice\n
in faciem\' have rather different wording so textual comparison gives\n
no help here=}\n

{=Reichl (1973) and CB13 and Woolf (1968 - see reference in Reichl)\n
all read HEUIn apparently for "heaven". This interpretation is made\n
